Title: Homogenization of в„“_2-Adversarial Training in High-Dimensions: Exact Dynamics under Stochastic Gradient Descent

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2607.00207v1

Published Time: Thu, 02 Jul 2026 00:19:01 GMT

Number of Pages: 118

Markdown Content:
# Homogenization of в„“2-Adversarial Training in High-Dimensions: Exact Dynamics under Stochastic Gradient Descent 

## Fabrizzio Sabelli UniversitГ© de MontrГ©al 

## fabrizzio.sabelli@umontreal.ca 

## July 2, 2026 

Abstract 

We develop a framework for analyzing the learning dynamics of в„“2-adversarial training of single-index models on Gaussian mixtures in the high-dimensional limit under streaming stochastic gradient descent (SGD). We derive deterministic equivalents for a broad class of statistics of the SGD iterates, including the adversarial risk and distance to adversarial optimality, in terms of the solution to a system of ODEs. We use them to study two idealized learning rate schedules: the Polyak stepsize and exact line search. In the case of в„“2-adversarial least squares with a single class, we show that, unlike noiseless standard least squares, no constant learning rate guarantees monotone descent of SGD towards a minimizer of the adversarial risk. We identify anisotropic covariance and a mismatch in ridge parameters as the main sources of suboptimality of exact line search relative to the Polyak stepsize. We also introduce a stochastic differential equation (SDE), called adversarial homogenized SGD, that captures the evolution of statistics of the iterates of SGD. For в„“2-adversarial least squares, using this SDE, we show the evolution of the risk is equivalent, up to dimension-free constants, to that of SGD on standard least squares with an adaptive learning rate and adaptive в„“2-regularization. When the dynamics converge, the limiting adversarial risk and SGD iterate are determined by a fixed-point equation, with the limiting iterate being equivalent to the solution of a ridge regression problem whose regularization parameter is the limiting effective regularization of SGD. 

# 1 Introduction 

State-of-the-art machine learning models have been shown to be vulnerable to small input perturbations, better known as adversarial examples [ 29 , 34 , 37 , 61 , 71 ]. These perturbations, often imperceptible to the human eye, are designed to maximize the modelвҖҷs error and result in large drops in model performance. Adversarial training has emerged as one of the most effective and persistent defenses against such perturbations [6, 34 , 44 ]. It consists of training on adversarial examples in order to increase the robustness of the model to unseen adversarial examples. It replaces the standard risk with a robust risk in which each sample is evaluated against its worst-case perturbation. When minimizing this objective with stochastic gradient descent (SGD), the update depends not only on the current iterate and the newly sampled data point, but also on the adversarial perturbation of this data point, thereby modifying the training dynamics of SGD. This dependence manifests empirically in the higher sensitivity of adversarial training to optimization hyperparameters, including the learning rate schedule, training time, weight decay, and early stopping [21, 47, 57]. In this work, we analyze the dynamics of adversarial training of generalized linear models under в„“2-adversarial perturbations and SGD. We extend the frameworks developed in [ 18 , 19 ] to account for adversarial training of single-index models. In these frameworks, the objective function is defined as follows

At each SGD update, a new data triplet (a, I, Пө ) is sampled such that a belongs to class I and Пө вҲј N (0 , О· 2) is independent label noise. Our main goal is not to study the optimal method for computing adversarial examples, but rather to provide a framework for analyzing how the adversarial objective affects the dynamics of SGD. 

Main contributions. 

вҖў We study в„“2-adversarial training of single-index models when input dimension d is proportional to the number of samples under data generated from a Gaussian mixture with two classes. We derive a deterministic equivalent which consists of a system of d-dimensional coupled ODEs which allows us to predict the learning curve of SGD for general covariances and means. See Figure 1. As the dimension d increases, the adversarial risk RAdv (Xk)

concentrates around RAdv (t) with overwhelming probability (see Theorem 3.4). 

вҖў We introduce a SDE called Adversarial Homogenized SGD which captures the dynamics of SGD as d вҶ’ вҲһ , even for large learning rate or above the convergence threshold. For data sampled from a single class a вҲј N (0 , K ),using this SDE we show that the behaviour of the adversarial risk of в„“2-adversarial least squares is equivalent up to constants (independent of d) to the adversarial risk of SGD on standard least squares with an adaptive learning rate and adaptive в„“2-regularization parameter. 

вҖў We analyze the deterministic equivalents to characterize the optimal learning rate ( Polyak stepsize ) which guarantees descent towards XвӢҶ, Adv вҲҲ argmin X Radv (X) and the optimal learning rate ( exact line search ) which guarantees decrease in RAdv at the next SGD update. For в„“2-adversarial least squares with data sampled from a single class a вҲј N (0 , K ), we show that no constant learning rate guarantees monotone descent of SGD towards 

XвӢҶ, Adv , in contrast to noiseless standard least squares. For a given state of the deterministic equivalents, we identify the two main factors driving suboptimality of exact line search in comparison to the Polyak stepsize: anisotropic covariance and a mismatch in ridge parameters (see (51)).

# 2 Related Work 

Adversarial training. Adversarial robustness and examples have been extensively studied in machine learning [ 6, 34 , 40 , 44 , 61 , 71 ]. Adversarial training has also been studied extensively in the case of deep neural networks [15 , 34 , 44 , 57 , 60 , 72 ]. From a theoretical perspective, the generalization and regularization properties of adversarial training as well as the robust versus standard accuracy trade-off have been studied in linear models [ 16 , 23 , 51 , 54 вҖ“ 56 , 69 ]. Under Gaussian data, exact asymptotics for these properties are derived in the proportional regime [ 27 , 36 , 39 , 63 , 65 ]. These properties were also investigated for Gaussian mixture models (GMM) [ 9, 23 , 26 , 38 , 62 ]. Using the theory of stochastic modified equations [ 42 ], [ 35 ] derive a SDE which approximates the min-max optimization dynamics under SGD in the vanishing learning rate limit. Our diffusion approximation of SGD differs from [ 35 ] as it does not rely on a vanishing stepsize but rather the input dimension d вҶ’ вҲһ .

Deterministic dynamics of stochastic algorithms in high-dimensions. As mentioned in [ 18 , 20 ], the theory of deterministic dynamics of stochastic algorithms on isotropic Gaussian data has a long history [ 10 , 11 , 58 , 59 ]. These rigorous results were also extended to other models [ 1, 2, 22 , 24 , 31 , 66 ] under isotropic Gaussian data. Deterministic equivalents were also proven for multi-pass SGD with small mini-batches [ 49 ] and with momentum [ 41 ]. Other high-dimensional deterministic equivalents yielding different types of dynamics have also been studied [ 3, 12 вҖ“14 , 28 , 30 , 46 ]. The works [ 4 , 8, 52 ] study the high-dimensional training dynamics of streaming SGD under GMM with isotropic covariance and the work [46] studies the analogous setting under multi-pass SGD. In recent years, a line of work has made significant contributions to understanding the effect of non-identity covariance on the training dynamics of SGD under data from a single class [ 7, 17 , 19 , 32 , 33 , 45 , 68 , 70 ]. As mentioned in [ 19 ], the non-identity covariance modifies the optimization landscape and affects convergence properties. The work [ 18 ] extends [ 19 ] to Gaussian mixture data with non-identity covariance and where the number of classes grows logarithmically with the input dimension d. Our work extends [ 18 , 19 ] by accounting for the в„“2-adversarial objective under Gaussian mixture data with two classes and non-identity covariance.

53 Deterministic Dynamics for SGD under в„“2-Adversarial Training

Note that it is possible to relax the boundedness assumption on the deterministic equivalents of the least squares risks and squared в„“2-norms by introducing an analogous theory of the set U (see Assumption 4) for HSGD. Under the assumptions presented in Propositions 6.1, 6.4 and 6.5 and Corollaries 6.1 and 6.2, for isotropic covariance and power law covariance when d вҶ’ вҲһ (See Section 6.1), the deterministic equivalents of the risk and squared в„“2-norm remain bounded from above and below by constants independent of dimension. Using an almost identical argument, we could show this holds in the case of R(t) and ОҰ( t). It follows that, under the assumptions presented in these Corollaries and up to constants independent of d, Proposition 4.1 holds. From Theorem 7.6 and similar arguments to Theorem 4 in [19] and Theorem 2.1 in [20], we can translate this back to SGD. 

# 5 Idealized Exact Line Search and Polyak Stepsize 

In this section, we consider the idealized algorithms: exact line search and Polyak stepsize . See [ 20 ] for a discussion of stochastic line search and Polyak-type stepsizes. Let D2,Adv (t) be the deterministic equivalent of вҲҘXвҢҠtd вҢӢ вҲ’ XвӢҶ, Adv вҲҘ2

where we recall XвӢҶ, Adv = argmin X RAdv (X). In deterministic optimization, the Polyak stepsize is the learning rate schedule chosen for maximum decrease of the distance to optimality at the following iteration. Exact line search is the learning rate strategy chosen for maximum decrease of the objective function at the next iteration. Analogously to [ 20 ], given that RAdv (XвҢҠtd вҢӢ) and вҲҘXвҢҠtd вҢӢ вҲ’ XвӢҶ, Adv вҲҘ2 are close to RAdv (t) and D2,Adv (t) when 

d вҶ’ вҲһ (See Theorem 3.4), we can ask this to hold for the deterministic equivalents. We restrict the results in this section to в„“2-adversarial least squares (see start of Section 4) as the general expressions derived are too cumbersome. See Appendix C and D for general results and the proofs. Given that we do not have access to XвӢҶ nor XвӢҶ, Adv nor distributional knowledge of a in practice, these are idealized algorithms. Nevertheless, they serve as a foundation for developing more practical algorithms.

For a fixed ODE state, the three adversarial regimes (see Proposition 6.3 and [ 69 , Proposition 1]) determine how strongly this anisotropy can affect the same-state comparison. If Оҙ вүӨ ОҙвҲ’, then ЛңО»eff (t) вҶ’ ЛңО»вӢҶ = 0 , so the damping from the ridge term disappears near the optimum. This is the regime most analogous to standard least squares as strong anisotropy can make exact line search substantially more conservative than Polyak [ 20 ]. If ОҙвҲ’ < Оҙ < Оҙ +, then 

ЛңО»eff (t) вҶ’ ЛңО»вӢҶ вҲҲ (0 , вҲһ), so the ridge term partially damps the spectral discrepancy. If Оҙ вүҘ Оҙ+, then ЛңО»eff (t) вҶ’ вҲһ as t вҶ’ вҲһ so the spectral factor approaches one. In this large Оҙ regime, anisotropy no longer creates a large mismatch between line search and Polyak and any remaining discrepancy is driven by the ridge factor. See Figure 4 and Figure 5 for numerical evidence illustrating the impact of Оҙ and anisotropy on the convergence of the в„“2-adversarial risk.

The three conditions derived in Proposition 6.6 are sufficient conditions under which power law covariance with 

Оұ вүҘ 0 converges to the unique admissible fixed point. The isotropic condition is sharp. For 0 < Оұ < 1, the condition gives a dimension-free bound. The ОәОұ condition is more conservative near Оұ = 0 , but remains useful beyond Оұ = 1 .

# 7 SGD and AdvHSGD are Approximate Solutions 

Outline of the section. In this section, we will prove the main result Theorem 3.4. The section is divided as follows. Section 7.1 introduces the Doob decompositions of SGD and AdvHSGD and provides an overview of the necessary steps to prove SGD and AdvHSGD are approximate solutions to the integro-differential equation (17) .Section 7.2 introduces the theory of approximate solutions to the integro-differential equation (17) . It also includes proofs on the stability of approximate solutions and the proofs that AdvHSGD and SGD are approximate solutions to (17) . Section 7.3 contains a rigorous justification for the removal of the stopping time in Theorems 3.1 and 3.2. Section 7.4 contains analogous results to Theorems 3.3 and 3.4 whenever Assumption 8 fails. Section 7.5 introduces the theory of approximate solutions to the integro-differential equation (183) and the rigorous justification for the removal of the stopping time in Theorems 3.3 and 3.4. Finally, Appendix A contains the details of the derivations of the Doob decompositions for AdvHSGD and SGD and supporting results for the proofs that AdvHSGD and SGD are approximate solutions to (17) . We choose to delegate these results to the Appendix as they are quite computational.

Remark 7.1. The results and proofs in this section will be presented for the soft label setting. Note that proofs may be adapted to the hard label setting by considering XвӢҶ = 0 and very similar arguments. 

Lemma 3 in [ 18 ] shows that up to constants depending on вҲҘKвҲҘ op , the norm вҲҘВ·вҲҘ О“ applied to the statistics Z(WвҢҠtd вҢӢ, В·)

(418) Adversarial training is designed to improve robust performance by minimizing the worst-case loss over admissible perturbations, but this can come at the expense of standard risk performance. This standard risk versus robust risk trade-off has been extensively studied empirically and theoretically. See Section 2 for more details. This brings us to the question: can this trade-off be controlled through the learning rate scheduling of adversarial training itself. In the context of our framework, this can be phrased as: given values of Оҙ, XвӢҶ and K, does there exist a learning rate schedule which minimizes the distance to optimality towards XвӢҶ? The thresholds (400) and (409) are pointwise stability thresholds along the SGD trajectory. In particular, descent towards XвӢҶ at iteration k is possible precisely when 

вҹЁXk вҲ’ XвӢҶ, вҲҮR Adv (Xk)вҹ© > 0. (419) This condition may hold along a particular path even when XвӢҶ is not the minimizer of RAdv which implies it is possible for adversarial training to move towards XвӢҶ over a region of state space despite the adversarial optimum satisfying XвӢҶ, Adv Мё = XвӢҶ. In order for (419) to hold globally on U (see Assumption 4), this requires вҲҮR Adv (XвӢҶ) = 0 

as shown in the following Lemma. 

Lemma C.1. Suppose RAdv is continuously differentiable at XвӢҶ then 

вҹЁXk вҲ’ XвӢҶ, вҲҮR Adv (Xk)вҹ© > 0 вҲҖ XМё = XвӢҶ =вҮ’ вҲҮR Adv (XвӢҶ) = 0 .

Proof. We proceed by proof by contradiction. Suppose вҲҮR Adv (XвӢҶ)Мё = 0 and for some Оө > 0, define XОө = XвӢҶ + Оөu for 

u = вҲ’ вҲҮR Adv (XвӢҶ)  

> вҲҘвҲҮR Adv (XвӢҶ)вҲҘ

. Then we have вҲҘXОө вҲ’ XвӢҶвҲҘ = Оө.Since RAdv is continuously differentiable at XвӢҶ then uвҠӨвҲҮR Adv (XОө) вҶ’ uвҠӨвҲҮR Adv (XвӢҶ) as Оө вҶ’ 0. Now, 

uвҠӨвҲҮR Adv (XвӢҶ) = вҲ’вҲҘвҲҮR Adv (XвӢҶ)вҲҘ < 0. By continuity, for sufficiently small Оө we have 

вҲҘR Adv (XОө) вҲ’ R Adv (XвӢҶ)вҲҘ < 12 вҲҘR Adv (XвӢҶ)вҲҘ.

Hence, we obtain 

uвҠӨвҲҮR Adv (XОө) = uвҠӨRAdv (XвӢҶ) + uвҠӨ(вҲҮR Adv (XОө) вҲ’ R Adv (XвӢҶ)) 

вүӨ вҲ’ 12 вҲҘвҲҮR Adv (XвӢҶ)вҲҘ < 0.

Thus, it follows that (XОө вҲ’ XвӢҶ)вҠӨвҲҮR Adv (XОө) = Оөu вҠӨRAdv (XОө) < 0 which is a contradiction. Sufficient conditions for вҹЁXk вҲ’ XвӢҶ, Adv , вҲҮR Adv (Xk)вҹ© > 0 for all XМё = XвӢҶ, Adv are either RAdv is convex and has a unique minimizer or RAdv is П…-strongly convex for some П… > 0.A consequence of Lemma C.1 is that if XвӢҶ, Adv is the unique minimizer fo RAdv then вҹЁXk вҲ’ XвӢҶ, вҲҮR Adv (Xk)вҹ© > 0

holding globally requires XвӢҶ, Adv = XвӢҶ. As proven in [ 69 ], in the case of в„“2-adversarial least squares with K вү» 0,

XвӢҶ, Adv = XвӢҶ whenever

# F Numerical Simulation Details
